Empty dropzone --></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="_i1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.8b08065/asset/images/medium/nn-2018-08065d_0007.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.8b08065/asset/images/large/nn-2018-08065d_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.8b08065&amp;id=_i1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">Sodium ion batteries are one of most promising alternatives to lithium ion batteries for large-scale energy storage, due to the high abundance and low cost of sodium in the earth. However, the lack of advanced electrode materials greatly affects their applications. Here, layered-structure SbPO<sub>4</sub> is explored as an anode material for sodium ion batteries in terms of SbPO<sub>4</sub> nanorods on reduced graphene oxide (SbPO<sub>4</sub>/rGO). <i>In situ</i> transmission electron microscopy images reveal the preferential expansion along the transverse direction of the nanorods upon the first discharging, which arises from the reduction of SbPO<sub>4</sub> to Sb and the subsequent alloying of Sb as supported by <i>in situ</i> X-ray diffraction and selected area electron diffraction patterns. SbPO<sub>4</sub>/rGO exhibits a capacity retention of 99% after 100 cycles at 0.5 A g<sup>–1</sup> both in half cells and in full cells. Its specific capacity at 5 A g<sup>–1</sup> is 214 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> in half cells or 134 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> in full cells. Moreover, the energy density of the full cells at 1.2 kW kg<sup>–1</sup><sub>total</sub> is still 99.8 W h kg<sup>–1</sup><sub>total</sub>, very promising as an advanced electrode material.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D12869%26pageCount%3D10%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DJun%2BPan%252C%2BShulin%2BChen%252C%2BQiang%2BFu%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D12%26issueNum%3D12%26contentID%3Dacsnano.8b08065%26title%3DLayered-Structure%2BSbPO4%252FReduced%2BGraphene%2BOxide%253A%2BAn%2BAdvanced%2BAnode%2BMaterial%2Bfor%2BSodium%2BIon%2BBatteries%26numPages%3D10%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1936-0851%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Dancac3%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D12878%26publicationDate%3DDecember%2B2018" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2018 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291952&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrochemical cells" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Electrochemical%20cells" class="article__tags__link">Electrochemical cells</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291371&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrodes" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Electrodes" class="article__tags__link">Electrodes</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291129&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Nanorods" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Nanorods" class="article__tags__link">Nanorods</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292439&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Physical and chemical processes" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Physical%20and%20chemical%20processes" class="article__tags__link">Physical and chemical processes</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292275&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Transmission electron microscopy" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Transmission%20electron%20microscopy" class="article__tags__link">Transmission electron microscopy</a></li></ul></div></div></div></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--keywords hlFld-Abstract"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Keywords</h3><div class="article__tags__tooltip" tabindex="0"><div class="article__tags__icon" aria-describedby="tooltip-keywords"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are keywords</span></div><p class="article__tags__diff" id="tooltip-keywords" role="tooltip">
                        Article keywords are supplied by the authors and highlight key terms and topics
                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Layered+Structure&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_layered%20structure">layered structure</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Antimony&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_antimony">antimony</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Phosphate&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_phosphate">phosphate</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Anode&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_anode">anode</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium+Ion+Batteries&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium%20ion%20batteries">sodium ion batteries</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="NLM_p first">Lithium ion batteries (LIBs), as high-efficiency energy storage devices, have achieved great successes in the past decades. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref1 ref2'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2" aria-label="reference 1 and 2">(1,2)</a> However, the limited resources of lithium, especially those available for exploitation, make the prospect of LIBs dim. Thus, sodium ion batteries (NIBs) are developed to address this concern and so far have attracted extensive attention. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref3 ref4'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref3 ref4" aria-label="reference 3 and 4">(3,4)</a> Although NIBs share the same cell configuration as that in LIBs, the electrode materials have to be re-explored because the larger diameter and heavier mass of Na<sup>+</sup> compared to those of Li<sup>+</sup> significantly degrade the electrochemical performances. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref5'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref5" aria-label="reference 5">(5)</a> Meanwhile, Na is chemically more active than Li, triggering more side reactions with electrolytes. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref6'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref6" aria-label="reference 6">(6)</a> Therefore, seeking suitable electrode materials for NIBs is very challenging. Graphite, the commercialized anode material in LIBs, is not a good candidate for NIBs <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref7'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref7" aria-label="reference 7">(7)</a> because its interlayer spacing is too small for Na<sup>+</sup>. Meanwhile, the small capacity and low initial Coulombic efficiency also impede its practical applications. Transition metal oxides do not work either, due to their large volume changes upon cycling and high working voltages (1–2 V). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref8 ref9'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref8 ref9" aria-label="reference 8 and 9">(8,9)</a> Recently, phosphorus has been indicated as an anode material for NIBs, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref10'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref10" aria-label="reference 10">(10)</a> due to its high theoretical capacity and low working voltage. However, large-scale processing of phosphorus may be dangerous because it can easily transform to highly flammable and toxic allotropes. Meanwhile, the composite of phosphorus/carbon further increases the fire risk.</div><div class="NLM_p">Compared to other candidates, anode materials based on alloying reactions always show high theoretical capacities. The typical examples include, but are not limited to, Sb, Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>, Sn, SnO<sub>2</sub>, SnS<sub><i>x</i></sub>, and so on. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref11 ref12 ref13 ref14'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref11 ref12 ref13 ref14" aria-label="reference 11 to 14">(11−14)</a> However, these materials are often subjected to substantial volume changes and large structure strain/stress upon cycling, which can easily induce structure deconstruction, particle pulverization, and thus performance degradation. In this context, how to maintain the structure upon cycling becomes the priority. Moreover, Na<sub>2</sub>O and Na<sub>2</sub>S generated in discharged electrodes are poorly conductive. Na<sub>2</sub>S is likely to be oxidized to polysulfides, followed by dissolving into electrolytes. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref15'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref15" aria-label="reference 15">(15)</a> Compared to oxides and sulfides, phosphates have received much less attention <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref16 ref17 ref18'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16 ref17 ref18" aria-label="reference 16 to 18">(16−18)</a> but do show promising potential as anode materials. First, bulky and stable PO<sub>4</sub><sup>3–</sup> anions can cushion the volume change upon cycling, thus promoting the cycling stability. Although Na<sub>2</sub>O and Na<sub>2</sub>S <i>in situ</i> generated during the sodiation process may have the similar effect, the enhancement is not comparable to Na<sub>3</sub>PO<sub>4</sub> in theory. Meanwhile, Na<sub>3</sub>PO<sub>4</sub> generated by the discharge reaction was reported to be an ionic conductor. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref19'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref19" aria-label="reference 19">(19)</a> Therefore, it may reduce the diffusion barrier of Na<sup>+</sup> and promote the reaction kinetics. Second, Sb, Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Sb, Sn, or Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Sn generated during the sodiation process can lead to three-dimensional electron pathways because they are either metals or intermetallics. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref20 ref21'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20 ref21" aria-label="reference 20 and 21">(20,21)</a> Third, phosphates have a variety of crystal structures that are electrochemically active, such as layered structure (SbPO<sub>4</sub>), NASICON structure (NaSn<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>), <i>etc</i>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref15 ref17 ref18'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref15 ref17 ref18" aria-label="reference 15, 17 and 18">(15,17,18)</a> Because the structure of electrode materials has a significant effect on the electrochemical performances, the test of phosphates in different structures is necessary and important. All of these features together make the exploration on phosphates attractive.</div><div class="NLM_p">Here, monoclinic-phase SbPO<sub>4</sub> is developed as the target material to illustrate the promising potential of phosphates in NIBs. In this phase, PO<sub>4</sub> and SbO<sub>4</sub> tetrahedra connect together at the corners, resulting in a layered structure (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>a). These layers stack along <i>a</i>-axis with an interlayer spacing of 5.087 Å. In order to improve the electrochemical performances of SbPO<sub>4</sub>, it is deposited to reduced graphene oxide (rGO) by a simple process, denoted as SbPO<sub>4</sub>/rGO. <i>Ex situ</i> characterization techniques, such as high-resolution transmission electron microscope (HRTEM) images and selected area electron diffraction (SAED) patterns, and <i>in situ</i> characterization techniques, such as X-ray diffraction (XRD), transmission electron microscopy (TEM), and SAED are measured to reveal the electrochemical reactions and morphology change of this composite upon cycling. Meanwhile, the electrochemical performances of SbPO<sub>4</sub>/rGO are evaluated both in half cells and in full cells. It presents an excellent cycling stability, with 99% capacity retention after 100 cycles at 0.5 A g<sup>–1</sup>. The full cells paired with homemade Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>/C give a high output voltage at 2.6 V, resulting in an energy density of 99.8 W h kg<sup>–1</sup><sub>total</sub> at 1.2 kW kg<sup>–1</sup><sub>total</sub>. As an anode material, these data make it very attractive in the future.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.8b08065/asset/images/medium/nn-2018-08065d_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.8b08065/asset/images/large/nn-2018-08065d_0001.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. Crystal structure and morphology of SbPO<sub>4</sub>/rGO. (a) Crystal structure of SbPO<sub>4</sub>. (b) XRD pattern, (c–e) XPS spectra, (f) FE-SEM, (g) TEM image, (h) HRTEM image, (i–l) element maps of Sb, P, O, and C, and (m) corresponding HAADF-STEM image.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.8b08065/asset/images/large/nn-2018-08065d_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.8b08065&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">SbPO<sub>4</sub>/rGO was synthesized by a solvothermal reaction between SbCl<sub>3</sub> and NH<sub>4</sub>H<sub>2</sub>PO<sub>4</sub> in the presence of GO, followed by an annealing in Ar/H<sub>2</sub> (see the <a class="ref internalNav" href="#sec4" aria-label="Experimental Section">Experimental Section</a>). <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>b shows the XRD pattern of the resultant product, where all the diffraction peaks could be indexed as monoclinic-phase SbPO<sub>4</sub> (JCPDS Card No. 35-0829). No diffraction peaks or bumps assigned to GO or rGO can be observed, probably due to the low contents and/or poor crystallinity. To clarify this point, Raman spectra (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.8b08065/suppl_file/nn8b08065_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a>) were measured, where two peaks at ∼1435 and ∼1580 cm<sup>–1</sup> assigned to the D-band and the G-band of carbon are readily observed. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref22'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22" aria-label="reference 22">(22)</a> It is believed that GO is reduced to rGO during the synthesis, due to the annealing in Ar/H<sub>2</sub> at a high temperature. Such a reduction is directly confirmed by Fourier transform infrared spectra (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.8b08065/suppl_file/nn8b08065_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a>). In this context, the final product is denoted as SbPO<sub>4</sub>/rGO. Thermogravimetric analysis (TGA) (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.8b08065/suppl_file/nn8b08065_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figures S3 and S4</a>) shows that the content of rGO in SbPO<sub>4</sub>/rGO is approximately ∼14.3 wt %. X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) was conducted to reveal the surface species of SbPO<sub>4</sub>/rGO. The signals of Sb, P, O, and C could be easily observed in the survey spectrum (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.8b08065/suppl_file/nn8b08065_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5</a>). The high-resolution spectra of these elements confirm the formation of SbPO<sub>4</sub>/rGO. As illustrated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>c, the peaks at 530.8 and 540.2 eV are assigned to Sb 3d<sub>5/2</sub> and Sb 3d<sub>3/2</sub> of SbPO<sub>4</sub>, and the peak at 531.9 eV is ascribed to O 1s. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref23'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23" aria-label="reference 23">(23)</a> The similar overlapping between Sb 3d and O 1s has been reported in previous works. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref24'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24" aria-label="reference 24">(24)</a> The peak at 134.5 eV in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>d is assigned to P 2p from P–O in PO<sub>4</sub><sup>3–</sup>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref25'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25" aria-label="reference 25">(25)</a> Different from the spectra of P 2p and Sb 3d, there are four peaks in the C 1s spectra (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>e), which are 284.6 eV from sp<sup>2</sup>-bonded C–C, 284.9 eV from sp<sup>3</sup>-bonded C–C, 286.2 eV from C–O, and 289.6 eV from O═C–OH. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref26'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26" aria-label="reference 26">(26)</a> The presence of C–O, C═O, and O═C–OH offers numerous anchoring sites for electrochemically active materials and then inhibits particle agglomeration or drop-off from electrodes.</div><div class="NLM_p">Then, SbPO<sub>4</sub>/rGO was characterized by field-emission scanning electron microscopy (FE-SEM) and TEM. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>f shows that numerous nanoparticles are well dispersed on a sheet-like structure, indicating the formation of SbPO<sub>4</sub>/rGO. The high-magnification TEM image (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>g) reveals that there are many short nanorods, with a diameter of 50 nm and length up to 350 nm. It is also noted that there is a small quantity of ellipsoids in SbPO<sub>4</sub>/rGO. The high-resolution TEM (HRTEM) image on a single nanorod (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>h) exhibits clear lattice fringes throughout the entire nanorod, indicating a single-crystal nature. The fringes with their interlayer spacings at 0.339 and 0.362 nm correspond to (020) and (−101) planes of SbPO<sub>4</sub>, respectively. The fast Fourier transform pattern, as shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.8b08065/suppl_file/nn8b08065_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6</a>, suggests that the nanorods grow along the direction of [−101], parallel to the polyhedron layers of SbPO<sub>4</sub>. Energy-dispersive X-ray spectra (EDS) (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.8b08065/suppl_file/nn8b08065_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7</a>) confirm the molar ratio of Sb to P at almost 1:1, close to that in SbPO<sub>4</sub>. To figure out the distributions of these elements, high-angle annular dark-field scanning TEM (HAADF-STEM) images and element mapping were conducted. As presented in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>i–m, the patterns of Sb, P, and O are well overlapped with that of nanorods, strongly supporting the formation of SbPO<sub>4</sub> nanorods. Meanwhile, the signal of carbon from rGO is also observed in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>l. All of these results confirm that SbPO<sub>4</sub> nanorods are attached to rGO. This good contact would benefit the charge transfer between rGO and SbPO<sub>4</sub> and restrain the particle pulverization upon cycling, thereby improving rate capability and cycling stability. N<sub>2</sub> sorption isotherms (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.8b08065/suppl_file/nn8b08065_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8</a>) suggest that SbPO<sub>4</sub>/rGO has a specific surface area of about 50.8 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup> and an average pore size of about 30 nm.</div><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>a shows the discharge/charge profiles of SbPO<sub>4</sub> at the first cycle. There is a pronounced voltage plateau at 0.9 V, indicating a typical two-phase process. After that, the voltage profile is followed by a shallow slope over 0.6–0.25 V. Compared to the discharge profile, only one short voltage slope between 0.5 and 0.75 V is observed in the first charge. In view of specific capacity and average voltage, it is believed that this voltage slope corresponds to the reverse reaction that happens over 0.5–0.3 V in the first discharge. To identify these electrochemical reactions, <i>in situ</i> XRD patterns and <i>ex situ</i> HRTEM images/SAED patterns are measured. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>b, the diffraction peaks at 24 and 38.5° can be attributed to conductive carbon and the current foil, Al, which do not alter in intensity and in peak positions upon cycling. Different from the cases of C and Al, the diffraction peaks of SbPO<sub>4</sub> gradually weaken with discharging and almost disappear below 0.8 V. This result can be speculated as the reduction reaction from SbPO<sub>4</sub> to metallic Sb. However, no obvious peaks from metallic Sb and Na<sub>3</sub>PO<sub>4</sub> are observed, which is likely caused by their poor crystallinity/small size. The similar phenomenon is very common in the products from conversion reactions. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref14 ref26 ref27'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref14 ref26 ref27" aria-label="reference 14, 26 and 27">(14,26,27)</a> Although metallic Sb is invisible in XRD patterns, its appearance is confirmed by <i>ex situ</i> SAED patterns (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>c) and HRTEM images (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>f), strongly supporting the reduction reaction. As the discharge process proceeds, metallic Sb is alloyed with Na, generating NaSb and Na<sub>3</sub>Sb in sequence. At the end of the discharge process, Na<sub>3</sub>Sb is easily identified in the XRD patterns (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>b), SAED pattern (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>d), and HRTEM image (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>g). Then, the electrode is charged back, where the diffraction peaks of Na<sub>3</sub>Sb shift the high angles and become weak (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>b). This result indicates the contraction of unit cells and the damage of crystal structures. At ∼0.8 V, these diffraction peaks almost disappear from the XRD pattern. Similar to the case of the discharge process, the diffraction peaks from metallic Sb are absent in the XRD pattern, but its diffraction rings and lattice fringes are confirmed by SAED patterns (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>e) and HRTEM images (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>h). This result is kept in the XRD patterns, even as the electrode is charged to 1.5 V (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.8b08065/suppl_file/nn8b08065_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9</a>). These results confirm that the alloying reaction, rather than the conversion reaction, contributes to the reversible capacity over 0.01–1.5 V, well explaining the low initial Coulombic efficiency of SbPO<sub>4</sub>. The involved electrochemical reactions upon cycling could be described by <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq1" aria-label="eqs 1">eqs </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq1" aria-label="1">1</a> and <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq2" aria-label="2">2</a>:<span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq1"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.8b08065/asset/images/medium/nn-2018-08065d_m001.gif" alt="" id="_i3"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(1)</span></div></div></span><span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq2"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.8b08065/asset/images/medium/nn-2018-08065d_m002.gif" alt="" id="_i4"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(2)</span></div></div></span></div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.8b08065/asset/images/medium/nn-2018-08065d_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.8b08065/asset/images/large/nn-2018-08065d_0002.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. Evolution of SbPO<sub>4</sub>/rGO during the sodiation/desodiation processes. (a) Galvanostatic discharge/charge profiles at the first cycle. (b) <i>In situ</i> XRD patterns, (c–e) <i>ex situ</i> SAED patterns, and (f–h) HRTEM images at different states of discharge/charge.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.8b08065/asset/images/large/nn-2018-08065d_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.8b08065&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">As the voltage window is expanded to 0.01–2.5 V, there is one more pair of voltage plateaus, 1.7 V in the discharge process and 2.2 V in the charge process (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.8b08065/suppl_file/nn8b08065_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S10</a>). The HRTEM images and SAED patterns of the electrode charged to 2.5 V (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.8b08065/suppl_file/nn8b08065_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S11</a>) confirm the formation of SbPO<sub>4</sub> again. Thus, it is believed that the new voltage plateaus originate from the redox reaction between Sb and SbPO<sub>4</sub>. However, the capacity contributed by this reaction (∼90 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>) is much smaller than the expected one (∼366 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>), indicating that this reaction is partially reversible. The high capacity of SbPO<sub>4</sub>/rGO over 0.01–2.5 V only lasts for 80 cycles (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.8b08065/suppl_file/nn8b08065_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S12</a>). After that, the capacity decays quickly, due to the larger structure stain/stress caused by the insertion/extraction of more Na ions. In this regard, the electrochemical test in this work is limited within the voltage window of 0.01–1.5 V.</div><div class="NLM_p"><i>In situ</i> TEM technique could intuitively reflect the changes of individual electrode particles in morphology and structure with nanometer/atomic resolution. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref28 ref29'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28 ref29" aria-label="reference 28 and 29">(28,29)</a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a shows the configuration of <i>in situ</i> TEM imaging, where SbPO<sub>4</sub> nanorods and metallic Na were employed as the working electrode and the counter electrode, respectively. The thin layer of Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>O on Na acted as a solid-state electrolyte, so sodium ions gradually passed through this layer and diffused into SbPO<sub>4</sub>. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>b shows the real-time TEM images upon discharge, where the nanorod expands anisotropically during sodiation; that is, the length of the SbPO<sub>4</sub> nanorod increases slightly (∼23 nm) upon discharging, but the diameter expands violently (∼90 nm) (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.8b08065/suppl_file/nn8b08065_si_002.avi" class="ext-link">Movie S1</a> and <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>c). This anisotropic volume change is likely associated with the unique layered structure of SbPO<sub>4</sub> that is aligned along the longitudinal direction of nanorods. Thus, as Na ions diffuse into the interlayers during the sodiation process (right side of <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a), SbPO<sub>4</sub> nanorods would preferentially expand along the transverse direction. The <i>in situ</i> SAED patterns in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>d were also recorded to track the structural evolution during the discharge process. The diffraction rings are radially integrated to intensity profiles in order to increase the accuracy (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.8b08065/suppl_file/nn8b08065_si_003.avi" class="ext-link">Movie S2</a>, Supporting Information). Both the position and intensity of the diffraction peaks vary a lot with the sodiation process, revealing the phase transition from SbPO<sub>4</sub> to Sb, NaSb, and eventually to Na<sub>3</sub>Sb (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>e). These results disclose the stepwise alloying of Sb during sodiation, in excellent agreement with <i>ex situ</i> TEM images.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.8b08065/asset/images/medium/nn-2018-08065d_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.8b08065/asset/images/large/nn-2018-08065d_0003.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. <i>In situ</i> TEM imaging of the sodiation process of SbPO<sub>4</sub>. (a) Schematic illustration on electrochemical setup for <i>in situ</i> TEM imaging, where metallic Na was taken as the counter electrode and a thin layer of Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>O on Na was used as the solid-state electrolyte. (b) Morphology changes of a single nanorod by <i>in situ</i> TEM. (c) Size changes in the longitudinal and transverse directions over time. (d) Phase evolutions of another nanostructure by <i>in situ</i> SAED pattern. (e) Corresponding radially integrated intensity profiles from a series of time-sequence SAED patterns.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.8b08065/asset/images/large/nn-2018-08065d_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.8b08065&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a shows the cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves of SbPO<sub>4</sub>/rGO during the first four cycles over 0.01–1.5 V at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. In the first cycle, the cathodic peak at 0.75 V is related to the reduction of SbPO<sub>4</sub> to metallic Sb, the decomposition of electrolytes, and the formation of a solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) film. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref4 ref30'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref4 ref30" aria-label="reference 4 and 30">(4,30)</a> After that, as-formed Sb alloys with Na, producing a cathodic peak at ∼0.25 V, consistent with that reported for this alloying reaction. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref31'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref31" aria-label="reference 31">(31)</a> In the first-charge process, there is only one broad and strong anodic peak at ∼0.75 V, which can be attributed to the desodiation of Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Sb alloy. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref32'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32" aria-label="reference 32">(32)</a> This result is also in line with those from <i>in situ</i> XRD patterns and <i>in situ</i> SAED patterns. In the second cycle, the cathodic peak at 0.75 V vanishes, but another peak at 0.4 V shows up, which also originates from the alloying reaction of Sb. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref33'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33" aria-label="reference 33">(33)</a> The cathodic peak at 0.22 V probably comes from residual Sb that did not react completely during the first discharge. Compared to the changes on the cathodic peaks, that of the anodic peak is quite small. In the third cycle, the cathodic peaks at 0.4 and 0.22 V merge together. From then on, the CV curves become almost identical, implying a good electrochemical stability. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>b shows the discharge/charge profiles of SbPO<sub>4</sub>/rGO at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup> for the first four cycles. The voltage profiles are in good agreement with the cathodic/anodic peaks in the CV curves. The first discharge/charge capacity is 766/309 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, resulting in an initial Coulombic efficiency of 40%. The low Coulombic efficiency comes from irreversible reactions during the first discharging, such as the formation of a SEI film, the irreversible conversion between SbPO<sub>4</sub> and metallic Sb, and so on. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref34 ref35'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34 ref35" aria-label="reference 34 and 35">(34,35)</a> However, after that, the Coulombic efficiency quickly rises to 99.5% and the capacity remains at 306 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, very close to the theoretical capacity of this composite (330.4 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> = 372 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> × 0.86 + 75 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> × 0.14) (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.8b08065/suppl_file/nn8b08065_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S13</a>). This result indicates that most of the active material in this composite has been well utilized in a high efficiency. It is noted that the low initial Coulombic efficiency can be improved by presodiation or wide voltage window (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.8b08065/suppl_file/nn8b08065_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S14</a>). <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>c displays the cycling performances of SbPO<sub>4</sub>/rGO and SbPO<sub>4</sub> within 0.01–1.5 V, using 1 M NaClO<sub>4</sub> in propylene carbonate as the electrolyte. Both of them were activated at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup> for the first three cycles and then cycled at 0.5 A g<sup>–1</sup>. Apparently, SbPO<sub>4</sub>/rGO exhibits a much better cycling stability than SbPO<sub>4</sub>. After 100 cycles at 0.5 A g<sup>–1</sup>, SbPO<sub>4</sub>/rGO still delivers a capacity of 280 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, much higher than 20 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> of SbPO<sub>4</sub>. This result indicates that rGO greatly improves the cycling stability. Unfortunately, the nanorods are not preserved after cycles (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.8b08065/suppl_file/nn8b08065_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S15</a>), probably due to the repeated deformation upon cycling, as illustrated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>. Despite this, active materials are still firmly attached to carbon, maintaining the electrochemical activity.</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.8b08065/asset/images/medium/nn-2018-08065d_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.8b08065/asset/images/large/nn-2018-08065d_0004.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. Electrochemical performances of SbPO<sub>4</sub>/rGO. (a) CV curves. (b) Discharge/charge profiles of SbPO<sub>4</sub>/rGO at a current density of 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>. (c) Cycling performance. (d) Simulated interface of Sb/rGO. (e) Long cycling performance.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.8b08065/asset/images/large/nn-2018-08065d_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.8b08065&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The excellent cycling performance can be attributed to the aggregation resistance of the Sb-containing particles, which comes from their secure attachment to rGO. First-principles calculations revealed that there are strong interactions at the interface region. Here, the (012) plane of Sb was adopted to simulate the contact surface of the particles. As revealed by our first-principles simulations, most of the contribution to the interaction between rGO and Sb come from van der Waals forces. The nature of such an interaction indicates that the bonding would not depend on the particular composition and termination of the Sb-containing particles. Therefore, we expect that the physical picture of the bonding patterns obtained on (012) planes can also be extended to other crystal facets including but not limited to (104) and (100) planes. Meanwhile, C–O groups were used as the anchor sites on rGO. More details about the computational model are described in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.8b08065/suppl_file/nn8b08065_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figures S16 and S17</a>. Interestingly, even in the absence of the anchor sites, the binding energy of Sb (012) on rGO is ∼2.62 eV, which originates from the van der Waals interaction. Upon the involvement of the anchor sites, the binding energy further increases to 3.26 eV, owing to the chemical bonds between Sb and O/C atoms (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>d). It is worth noting that the value of 3.26 eV is a lower limit because, in reality, the fluctuation of the rGO geometry is more flexible than that in the simulations, which could result in more effective bonding patterns. Overall, the van der Waals interactions lead to the deposition of the particles, and the formation of the chemical bonds effectively prevents these particles from diffusing and aggregating on rGO, both of which, cooperatively, stabilize the working units of the battery. This strong interaction is also supported by XPS spectra of SbPO<sub>4</sub> and SbPO<sub>4</sub>/rGO discharged to 0.5 V, where Sb was <i>in situ</i> generated. Because Sb 3d is always interfered by O 1s, Sb 4d is used to confirm this point. As shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.8b08065/suppl_file/nn8b08065_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S18</a>, Sb 4d of the discharged electrode from SbPO<sub>4</sub>/rGO shifts to the high binding energies, as compared to that from SbPO<sub>4</sub>. This shift indicates the decreasing of electron density around Sb, which is in good agreement with the interaction between Sb and rGO. Encouraged by these results, the long-term cycling of SbPO<sub>4</sub>/rGO at a high rate was measured. As presented in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>e, SbPO<sub>4</sub>/rGO was cycled at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup> for the first 10 cycles and then at 1 A g<sup>–1</sup> for the following cycles. After 1000 cycles, the specific capacity still maintains at 100 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, corresponding to a capacity retention of 66%. Although this capacity is not impressive (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.8b08065/suppl_file/nn8b08065_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a>), the lifespan outperforms many Sb-related anodes. Moreover, if evaluated in terms of the cycling stability, SbPO<sub>4</sub>/rGO is also superior to many reports.</div><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>a shows the rate performance of SbPO<sub>4</sub>/rGO, where the specific capacity decreases from 323 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup> to 301 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.2 A g<sup>–1</sup>, 289 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.5 A g<sup>–1</sup>, 278 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 1 A g<sup>–1</sup>, 262 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 2 A g<sup>–1</sup>, and 214 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 5 A g<sup>–1</sup>. Even at a high current density of 10 A g<sup>–1</sup>, it still reaches 127 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>. The capacity retention of SbPO<sub>4</sub>/rGO is higher than that of SbPO<sub>4</sub> and most of the Sb-related materials (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.8b08065/suppl_file/nn8b08065_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S19</a>). To gain insights about this result, EIS spectra were measured at 1.5 V after 10 cycles and fitted by an equivalent circuit (the inset of <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>b). <i>R</i><sub>s</sub> indicates the resistance of the electrolyte and cell components. <i>R</i><sub>f</sub> is the resistance related to the surface film; <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> refers to the charge transfer resistance at the interfaces, and <i>Z</i><sub>w</sub> represents the Warburg impedance related to Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref22 ref36'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22 ref36" aria-label="reference 22 and 36">(22,36)</a> It is noted that both <i>R</i><sub>f</sub> and <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> of SbPO<sub>4</sub>/rGO are much smaller than those of SbPO<sub>4</sub> (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.8b08065/suppl_file/nn8b08065_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S2</a>), implying the enhanced reaction kinetics in SbPO<sub>4</sub>/rGO. Why SbPO<sub>4</sub>/rGO shows the superior performances in rate capability and cycling stability could be illustrated as follows. First, rGO and as-formed Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Sb and Sb offer three-dimensional highways for charge transfer and promote the electrochemical reactions. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref37'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37" aria-label="reference 37">(37)</a> Second, rGO and Na<sub>3</sub>PO<sub>4</sub> work as the buffers to volume change upon cycling, improving the cycling stability. Third, the strong interaction between Sb and rGO restrains the particle pulverization and aggregation during sodiation/desodiation processes. All of these features make the composite promising as an anode material.</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.8b08065/asset/images/medium/nn-2018-08065d_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.8b08065/asset/images/large/nn-2018-08065d_0005.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. Kinetics analysis of Na storage mechanism. (a) Rate performance. (b) EIS spectra. (c) CV curves of SbPO<sub>4</sub>/rGO at various scan rates. (d) Plots of log (scan rate) <i>versus</i> log (peak current). (e) Capacitive contribution in CV curves (shaded region) of SbPO<sub>4</sub>/rGO. (f) Contribution ratio of capacitive capacity in SbPO<sub>4</sub>/rGO at different sweep rates.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.8b08065/asset/images/large/nn-2018-08065d_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.8b08065&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Kinetics analysis was conducted on the basis of CV curves at different scan rates. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>c, these CV curves scanned from 0.2 to 2.0 mV s<sup>–1</sup> exhibit a similar contour. The relationship between the peak current (<i>i</i><sub>p</sub>) and the scan rate (<i>v</i>) could be described by <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq3" aria-label="eq 3">eq </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq3" aria-label="3">3</a>: <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref38'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38" aria-label="reference 38">(38)</a><span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq3"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.8b08065/asset/images/medium/nn-2018-08065d_m003.gif" alt="" id="_i5"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(3)</span></div></div></span>where <i>a</i> and <i>b</i> are empirical constants that could be obtained by a logarithmic plot of <i>i</i><sub>p</sub> with <i>v</i>, as displayed in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>d; <i>b</i> = 0.5 indicates an ideally diffusion-controlled electrochemical process, whereas <i>b</i> = 1.0 implies a typical surface-controlled electrochemical process. In most cases, <i>b</i> stays between 0.5 and 1.0, suggesting a mixed contribution from two processes. In SbPO<sub>4</sub>/rGO, <i>b</i> values of the cathodic and anode peaks are identified as 0.73 and 0.78. According to Dunn <i>et al.</i>, the ratio of the two contributions could be estimated from <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq4" aria-label="eq 4">eq </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq4" aria-label="4">4</a>: <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref39'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39" aria-label="reference 39">(39)</a><span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq4"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.8b08065/asset/images/medium/nn-2018-08065d_m004.gif" alt="" id="_i6"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(4)</span></div></div></span>Here, <i>i</i> is the current at a fixed potential. <i>k</i><sub>1</sub><i>v</i> and <i>k</i><sub>2</sub><i>v</i><sup>1/2</sup> represent the contributions of a surface-controlled process and a diffusion-controlled process, respectively. To simplify the calculation, this equation is reorganized by dividing both sides by <i>v</i><sup>1/2</sup>, giving <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq5" aria-label="eq 5">eq </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq5" aria-label="5">5</a>:<span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq5"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.8b08065/asset/images/medium/nn-2018-08065d_m005.gif" alt="" id="_i7"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(5)</span></div></div></span>In this context, <i>k</i><sub>1</sub> and <i>k</i><sub>2</sub> can be easily obtained as the slope and intercept point at the <i>y</i>-axis in a plot of <i>i</i>/<i>v</i><sup>1/2</sup><i>versus</i><i>v</i><sup>1/2</sup>. Thus, the ratio of a diffusion-controlled process and a surface-controlled process at this potential could be clarified. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref22 ref40'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22 ref40" aria-label="reference 22 and 40">(22,40)</a> Expanding this procedure to other potentials would disclose the contributions of the two processes to the total capacity. As illustrated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>e, the capacitive process contributes 79% of the total capacity at a scan rate of 2.0 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. These data gradually decrease to 44%, as the scan rate slows to 0.2 mV s<sup>–1</sup> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>f).</div><div class="NLM_p">In view of the excellent performances of SbPO<sub>4</sub>/rGO in half cells, it is tested in full cells by using homemade Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>/C as a cathode material (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.8b08065/suppl_file/nn8b08065_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S20</a>, Supporting Information). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref41'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref41" aria-label="reference 41">(41)</a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>a shows the scheme of the full cell. The full cell was tested within 1.2–3.4 V at a current density of 0.5 A g<sup>–1</sup><sub>anode</sub>. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>b, there is a pronounced voltage plateau at 2.6 V, and this result would benefit the practical applications in the future. The cycling performances are evaluated at 0.5 A g<sup>–1</sup><sub>anode</sub>, but the first five cycles were conducted at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup><sub>anode</sub> for electrochemical activation. As illustrated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>c, the full cell displays a very stable cycling. After 100 cycles at 0.5 A g<sup>–1</sup>, the capacity is still 190 mA h g<sup>–1</sup><sub>anode</sub>, corresponding to a capacity retention of almost 99.2%. The superior rate performances of SbPO<sub>4</sub>/rGO are also inherited in full cells (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>d). There is a specific capacity of 204 mA h g<sup>–1</sup><sub>anode</sub> for 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup><sub>anode</sub>, 202 mA h g<sup>–1</sup><sub>anode</sub> for 0.2 A g<sup>–1</sup>, 200 mA h g<sup>–1</sup><sub>anode</sub> for 0.5 A g<sup>–1</sup><sub>anode</sub>, 179 mA h g<sup>–1</sup><sub>anode</sub> for 2 A g<sup>–1</sup><sub>anode</sub>, and 136 mA h g<sup>–1</sup><sub>anode</sub> for 5 A g<sup>–1</sup><sub>anode</sub>. These results indicate that the full cells could be charged to 66.7% within 1.6 min. The electrode polarization slowly increases with the increasing rate (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>e). <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>f shows the energy density and power density of full cells calculated on the basis of total mass of active materials (Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>/C + SbPO<sub>4</sub>/rGO). It is found that the energy density decays slowly. Even at 1.2 kW kg<sup>–1</sup>, there is still an energy density of 99.8 W h kg<sup>–1</sup><sub>total</sub>, 81.3% of that at 0.06 kW kg<sup>–1</sup>. All these data indicate the promising potential of SbPO<sub>4</sub>/rGO as a high-performance anode material for Na ion batteries.</div><figure id="fig6" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.8b08065/asset/images/medium/nn-2018-08065d_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.8b08065/asset/images/large/nn-2018-08065d_0006.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. Electrochemical performances of the full cell (SbPO<sub>4</sub>/rGO //Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>/C). (a) Scheme of the full cell. (b) Discharge/charge curves. (c) Cycling performance at 0.5 A g<sup>–1</sup>. (d) Rate performance of the full cell. (e) Discharge/charge curves at different current density. Current density and specific capacity in (b–e) is calculated on the mass of the anode, SbPO<sub>4</sub>/rGO. (f) Ragone-like plot of the full cell. Power density and energy density here are calculated on the total mass of the anode and cathode materials, SbPO<sub>4</sub>/rGO + Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>/C.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.8b08065/asset/images/large/nn-2018-08065d_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.8b08065&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i8">Conclusion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">SbPO<sub>4</sub> nanorods are successfully deposited on reduce graphene oxide (SbPO<sub>4</sub>/rGO) by a simple solvothermal reaction, followed by a low-temperature annealing. A series of <i>ex</i>/<i>in situ</i> technologies confirm the stepwise alloying/dealloying of Sb to Na<sub>3</sub>Sb over 0.01–1.5 V. The unique alignment of a layered structure makes the volume expansion preferentially occur along the transverse direction of SbPO<sub>4</sub> nanorods, as supported by <i>in situ</i> TEM images. SbPO<sub>4</sub>/rGO presents a specific capacity of 323 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>. Even at 10 A g<sup>–1</sup>, a capacity is still approximately ∼127 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>. After 100 cycles at 0.5 A g<sup>–1</sup>, the capacity retention remains at ∼99%, confirming the outstanding cycle stability. The superior performances could be attributed to rGO in the composite, which effectively enhances the electron conductivity, promotes the accommodation to volume change, and inhibits the potential peel-off of Sb. Inspired by these results, SbPO<sub>4</sub>/rGO is evaluated in full cells by pairing with Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>/C. It works an average voltage at 2.6 V. After 100 cycles at 0.5 A g<sup>–1</sup>, the capacity retention is kept at 99.2%. Even at 1.2 kW kg<sup>–1</sup><sub>total</sub>, there is still an energy density of 99.8 W h kg<sup>–1</sup><sub>total</sub>, making it attractive in the future.</div></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i9">Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec4_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i10" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i10"> Material Synthesis</h3><div class="NLM_p last">GO was fabricated using a modified Hummers method. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref42'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref42" aria-label="reference 42">(42)</a> GO (30 mg) was dispersed in ethylene glycol (40 mL), followed by addition of SbCl<sub>3</sub> (228 mg) and NH<sub>4</sub>H<sub>2</sub>PO<sub>4</sub> (345 mg). This dispersion was heated to 70 °C and transferred to a Teflon-lined stainless autoclave. After being maintained at 160 °C for 4 h, the autoclave was cooled to room temperature naturally. The powders were collected by centrifuge, washed by distilled water and absolute ethanol, and dried at 60 °C overnight. Finally, the powders were annealed at 400 °C for 2 h in Ar/H<sub>2</sub> (95:5). This product, SbPO<sub>4</sub>/rGO, was used for structure characterization and electrochemical performances. SbPO<sub>4</sub> nanoparticles were prepared without rGO in the recipe and used as the benchmark to illustrate the role of rGO.</div></div><div id="sec4_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i11" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i11"> Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p last">Powder XRD patterns were measured on an X-ray diffractometer (Bruker D8 Advanced, Germany), using monochromatic Cu Kα as a radiation source. SEM images were taken from a field-emission scanning electron microscope (Zeiss SUPRA55, Germany), connected with an energy-dispersive X-ray spectrometer (Oxford Instruments). TEM images were acquired on an aberration-corrected transmission electron microscope (FEI Titan Themis G2, USA) operated at 300 kV. Raman spectra were obtained on a LabRAM HR800 Raman spectrometer, using an excitation at 632 nm at room temperature. XPS spectra were achieved from an X-ray photoelectron spectrometer (ESCA LAB 250, USA). TGA was conducted on a thermal analyzer (Mettler Toledo TGA/SDTA85, Canada) from room temperature to 800 °C in air. N<sub>2</sub> sorption isotherms were measured on a gas sorptometer (Micromeritics ASAP 2020 HD88, USA) at 77 K.</div></div><div id="sec4_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i12" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i12"> Electrochemical Measurements</h3><div class="NLM_p last">Electrochemical performances of SbPO<sub>4</sub> and SbPO<sub>4</sub>/rGO as anode materials in sodium ion batteries were evaluated in CR2032-type coin cells, using pure sodium foil as the counter electrode and glass fitter (whatman GF/F) as a separator. The working electrode was fabricated by spreading a slurry of SbPO<sub>4</sub> or SbPO<sub>4</sub>/rGO, acetylene black, and sodium alginate (SA) in a weight ratio of 7:2:1 on a copper foil. After the foil was dried in vacuum at 60 °C for 12 h, the electrode was cut into disks with diameters of 12 nm. The mass loading of active material was 1.0–1.5 mg cm<sup>–2</sup>. Then, the coin cells were assembled in an argon-filled glovebox (Mikrouna, Super 1220/750/900, H<sub>2</sub>O &lt; 1 ppm, O<sub>2</sub> &lt; 1 ppm), using 1.0 M NaClO<sub>4</sub> in propylene carbonate containing 3 wt % FEC as an electrolyte. For full cells, the cathode was made of homemade Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>/C, acetylene black, and poly(vinylidene) in a weight ratio of 8:1:1 deposited on an alumium foil. The anode was fabricated by the above protocol and electrochemically activated in a half cell before it was assembled into full cells. The capacity ratio between anode and cathode was controlled to about 1.2:1. In this case, the anode capacity slightly exceeds the cathode one, promoting the utilization of cathode materials and preventing the formation of dendrites. CVs were performed using an electrochemical workstation (LK 2005A, China) at room temperature. Galvanostatic discharge/charge profiles were obtained on battery cyclers (LAND CT-2001A, China) between 0.01 and 1.5 V at 25 °C. EIS spectra were taken on an electrochemical workstation (Autolab PGSTAT 302N) over a frequency range of 100 kHz to 0.01 Hz.</div></div><div id="sec4_4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i13" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i13"> <i>In Situ</i> TEM Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p last"><i>In situ</i> TEM images and SAED patterns were obtained out using Tecnai F20 at 200 kV equipped with an OneView IS (Gatan) camera. The <i>in situ</i> experiment was conducted using a simplified battery configuration with SbPO<sub>4</sub> dispersed onto a half-copper mesh as the working electrode and a piezo-driven tungsten tip coated by Na as the counter electrode. To achieve this fabrication, SbPO<sub>4</sub> was dispersed in a half-copper grid and transferred to a specially designed specimen holder (PicoFemto). Then, the W tip was used to scratch the surface of Na metal. All of the process was conducted in an argon-filled glovebox. Finally, the holder was transferred into a microscope column. During the transfer process, the Na was shortly exposed to the air to form a layer of Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>O on the surface of Na, serving as the solid electrolyte. The W tip is kept in touch with SbPO<sub>4</sub> by a piezo-ceramic manipulator. To initialize the sodiation, a bias of −2 V was applied on SbPO<sub>4</sub> against the counter electrode to promote Na ion transport through the Na<sub>2</sub>O layer.</div></div><div id="sec4_5" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i14" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i14"> Computation Details</h3><div class="NLM_p last">All calculations were performed using the Vienna ab initio simulation package (VASP). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref43 ref44'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref43 ref44" aria-label="reference 43 and 44">(43,44)</a> The energy cutoff for the plane-wave basis set was 400 eV, and optB86b-vdW was employed as the exchange-correlation functional. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref45 ref46'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref45 ref46" aria-label="reference 45 and 46">(45,46)</a> All atoms were allowed to relax until the maximum force was below 0.03 Å /eV. Dipole correction on the total energies was added along the perpendicular direction.</div></div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes1"><div                 10.1038/s41467-017-02808-2</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D9%26publication_year%3D2018%26pages%3D402%26journal%3DNat.%2BCommun.%26author%3DJ.%2BW.%2BHan%26author%3DD.%2BB.%2BKong%26author%3DW.%2BLv%26author%3DD.%2BM.%2BTang%26author%3DD.%2BL.%2BHan%26author%3DC.%2BZhang%26author%3DD.%2BH.%2BLiu%26author%3DZ.%2BC.%2BXiao%26author%3DX.%2BH.%2BZhang%26author%3DJ.%2BXiao%26author%3DX.%2BZ.%2BHe%26author%3DF.%2BC.%2BHsia%26author%3DC.%2BZhang%26author%3DY.%2BTao%26author%3DD.%2BGolberg%26author%3DF.%2BY.%2BKang%26author%3DL.%2BJ.%2BZhi%26author%3DQ.%2BH.%2BYang%26title%3DCaging%2BTin%2BOxide%2Bin%2BThree-Dimensional%2BGraphene%2BNetworks%2Bfor%2BSuperior%2BVolumetric%2BLithium%2BStorage%26doi%3D10.1038%252Fs41467-017-02808-2&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsnano.8b08065&amp;doiOfLink=10.1038%2Fs41467-017-02808-2&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit2R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">2</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Caging tin oxide in three-dimensional graphene networks for superior volumetric lithium storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Han Junwei; Han Daliang; Liu Donghai; Xiao Jing; He Xinzi; Tao Ying; Yang Quan-Hong; Kong Debin; Xiao Zhichang; Zhang Xinghao; Zhi Linjie; Lv Wei; Kang Feiyu; Tang Dai-Ming; Hsia Feng-Chun; Golberg Dmitri; Zhang Chao; Golberg Dmitri; Zhang Chen</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature communications</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2018</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">9</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">1</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">402</span>
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn"></span>.
    </div><div class="casAbstract">Tin and its compounds hold promise for the development of high-capacity anode materials that could replace graphitic carbon used in current lithium-ion batteries.  However, the introduced porosity in current electrode designs to buffer the volume changes of active materials during cycling does not afford high volumetric performance.  Here, we show a strategy leveraging a sulfur sacrificial agent for controlled utility of void space in a tin oxide/graphene composite anode.  In a typical synthesis using the capillary drying of graphene hydrogels, sulfur is employed with hard tin oxide nanoparticles inside the contraction hydrogels.  The resultant graphene-caged tin oxide delivers an ultrahigh volumetric capacity of 2123 mAh cm(-3) together with good cycling stability.  Our results suggest not only a conversion-type composite anode that allows for good electrochemical characteristics, but also a general synthetic means to engineering the packing density of graphene nanosheets for high energy storage capabilities in small volumes.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=LOZGTtr2ZcR-vwvzncD3aDAyhZbj3lKdfW6udTcc2eaW1a_jJi-aerntkvexoksv"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A280%3ADC%252BC1MvkslamsA%253D%253D&amp;md5=454ef54ca1f86954340cd84d4d7e9582</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref3'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">3</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit3" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.5b08274"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ji, L. W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, W. D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chabot, V.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yu, A. C.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Reduced Graphene Oxide/Tin–Antimony Nanocomposites as Anode Materials for Advanced Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2015</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>7</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">24895</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">24901</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1021/acsami.5b08274</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D7%26publication_year%3D2015%26pages%3D24895-24901%26journal%3DACS%2BAppl.%2BMater.%2BInterfaces%26author%3DL.%2BW.%2BJi%26author%3DW.%2BD.%2BZhou%26author%3DV.%2BChabot%26author%3DA.%2BP.%2BYu%26author%3DX.%2BC.%2BXiao%26title%3DReduced%2BGraphene%2BOxide%252FTin%25E2%2580%2593Antimony%2BNanocomposites%2Bas%2BAnode%2BMaterials%2Bfor%2BAdvanced%2BSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%26doi%3D10.1021%252Facsami.5b08274&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsnano.8b08065&amp;doiOfLink=10.1021%2Facsami.5b08274&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit3R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">3</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Reduced Graphene Oxide/Tin-Antimony Nanocomposites as Anode Materials for Advanced Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Ji, Liwen; Zhou, Weidong; Chabot, Victor; Yu, Aiping; Xiao, Xingcheng</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Applied Materials &amp; Interfaces</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">7</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">44</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">24895-24901</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">AAMICK</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1944-8244</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Reduced graphene oxides loaded with tin-antimony alloy (RGO-SnSb) nanocomposites were synthesized through a hydrothermal reaction and the subsequent thermal redn. treatments.  Transmission electron microscope images confirm that SnSb nanoparticles with an av. size of about 20-30 nm are uniformly dispersed on the RGO surfaces.  When they were used as anodes for rechargeable sodium (Na)-ion batteries, these as-synthesized RGO-SnSb nanocomposite anodes delivered a high initial reversible capacity of 407 mA h g-1, stable cyclic retention for more than 80 cycles and excellent cycle stability at ultra high charge/discharge rates up to 30C.  The significantly improved performance of the synthesized RGO-SnSb nanocomposites as Na-ion battery anodes can be attributed to the synergetic effects of RGO-based flexible framework and the nanoscale dimension of the SnSb alloy particles (&lt;30 nm).  Nanosized intermetallic SnSb compds. can exhibit improved structural stability and cond. during charge and discharge reactions compared to the corresponding individuals (Sn and Sb particles).  In the meantime, RGO sheets can tightly anchor SnSb alloy particles on the surfaces, which can not only effectively suppress the agglomeration of SnSb particles but also maintain excellent electronic conduction.  Furthermore, the mech. flexibility of the RGO phase can accommodate the vol. expansion and contraction of SnSb particles during the prolonged cycling, therefore, improve the electrode integrity mech. and electronically.  All of these contribute to the electrochem. performance improvements of the RGO-SnSb nanocomposite-based electrodes in rechargeable Na-ion batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoyfmdRHQjAR7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0li3rx9qn55mIA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXhslWjsb7M&amp;md5=eb22d9541ec0d95a7438fe4308e90e7a</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref4"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref4'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">4</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit4" data-doi="10.1021/acsnano.7b08714"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tian, W.                         cas_cit5R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">5</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Hard carbon nanoparticles as high-capacity, high-stability anodic materials for Na-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Xiao, Lifen; Cao, Yuliang; Henderson, Wesley A.; Sushko, Maria L.; Shao, Yuyan; Xiao, Jie; Wang, Wei; Engelhard, Mark H.; Nie, Zimin; Liu, Jun</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Energy</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">19</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">279-288</span>CODEN:
